The Maturidi Clarification on Act and Effect
Nasafi concludes by denying the union of act and effect from one aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the act of the servant is an effect of God and an act of the servant, without being identical in aspect.

Core Idea
The same entity can be an act from one perspective and an effect from another, but never both from the same aspect.

After the response from al-Qattan and al-Qalanisi, Imam Nasafi clarifies the Maturidi position. He asserts that the act of the servant is an effect (maf'ul) of God and an act (fi'l) of the servant, but these are different aspects. He denies that the same thing can be both act and effect from the same aspect. This lesson explains this nuanced position and how it answers the Mu'tazila objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the conclusion. Pay attention to the phrases 'من حيث إنه' (insofar as) indicating different aspects.
2
Identify the two aspects
The act is described as being an act of the servant and an effect of God from different aspects. Is there a contradiction?
3
Understand the denial of union
Nasafi explicitly says he denies the union of act and effect from one aspect. This is the key to resolving the objection.
This shows the Maturidi position is not that the act is literally both act and effect in the same sense.
4
Reflect on the implications
By distinguishing aspects, the Maturidis maintain that the servant truly acts (through acquisition) while God is the sole creator of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نظير هذا على قول من يسلم منكم أن العبد له فعل في الفرق بين فعل الفاعلين وكلام المتكلمين، فإن فعل العبد لما كان مخلوقا كان خلقا وفعلا الله تعالى فيكون فعلا الله تعالى وللعبد فيكون فعلا لفاعلين. فتدعى المعتزلة أن هذا محال، ويعتبرونه بكلام واحد أنه لا يكون كلاما لمتكلمين ويستحيل أن يكون ذلك فكذا هذا. أجابهم من سلم منكم أن للعبد فعلا وهو عبد الله بن سعيد القطان وأبو العباس القلانسي فقال: بأن الفعل اسم عام لكل ما هو مقدور مفعول، وقد ثبت أن فعلنا مقدور الله تعالى ومخلوقه فكان خلقه وفعله، والكلام اسم لفعل خاص وهو المعنى المنافي للسكوت والخرس والآفة، وهو ينافي سكوت المحل الذي قام به آفته ويستحيل قيامه بمحلين لنفي السكوت والآفة عن محلين فلم يكن كلاما لمحلين. فأما الفعل فهو اسم لما يقع بالقدرة وهو مقدور الله تعالى ومقدور العبد، فكان فعلا لهما، حتى إن الكلام لما كان يقع بقدرة الله خلقا وقدرة العبد كسبا كان أيضا فعلا لفاعلين أما لم يكن كلاهما لمتكلمين لما مر، فكذا الضرب عندنا لا يكون مضروبا لأنه اسم لنوع فعل وهو الإيلام والمضروب اسم للمتألم والضرب لا يقبل الألم فلم يكن مشروبا. فأما الضرب فإنه يقبل التخليق فكان مخلوقا مفعولا، فكان من حيث إنه فعل للعبد مفعولا لله تعالى، فأما من حيث إنه ضرب لم يكن مضروبا. ثم نحن إنما ندعي إحالة كون الفعل مفعولا لمن هو فعله، وإحالة كون المفعول فعلا لمن هو مفعوله وننكر اتحاد الفعل والمفعول وفيها ألزمت الأمر بخلافه، فإن فعل العبد عندنا كان مفعولا الله تعالى لا للعبد، وكان هو فعلا للعبد لا الله تعالى فمن كان هو فعلا له لم يكن مفعولا، ومن كان مفعولا له لم يكن فعلا له ونحن لا ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر اتحاد الفعل والمفعول بجهة واحدة وهو إضافته.
Then the parallel to this, according to the statement of one among you who concedes that the servant has an act, regarding the distinction between the act of two agents and the speech of two speakers: for the act of the servant, when it is created, is creation and act of God, so it is an act of God and of the servant, thus it is an act of two agents. The Mu'tazila claim this is impossible, and they analogize it to a single speech not being speech of two speakers, and that it is impossible; so likewise this. Those among you who concede that the servant has an act—namely Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi—replied to them, saying: 'Fi'l is a general name for everything that is possible and effected, and it has been established that our act is possible to God and His creation, so it is His creation and act. Kalam is a name for a specific act, namely the meaning opposed to silence, muteness, and defect, and it opposes the silence of the locus in which it inheres, and it is impossible for it to subsist in two loci because silence and defect are negated from two loci; thus it is not speech for two loci. As for fi'l, it is a name for what occurs by power, and it is possible to God and possible to the servant, so it is an act of both. Even kalam, when it occurs by God's power as creation and by the servant's power as acquisition, is also an act of two agents, but it is not speech of two speakers for the reason mentioned. So similarly, hitting according to us is not a hit object, because it is the name of a type of act, which is causing pain, and the hit object is the name of the one who suffers pain, and hitting does not receive pain, so it is not a hit object. As for hitting, it accepts being created, so it is created and effected; thus it is, insofar as it is an act of the servant, an effect of God; but insofar as it is hitting, it is not a hit object.' Then we only claim the impossibility of the act being an effect for the one for whom it is an act, and the impossibility of the effect being an act for the one for whom it is an effect, and we deny the union of act and effect. In this, they have imposed the contrary, for the act of the servant according to us is an effect of God, not of the servant, and it is an act of the servant, not of God. So that which is an act of Him is not an effect of Him, and that which is an effect of Him is not an act of Him. We do not deny this; we only deny the union of act and effect from one aspect, which is its attribution.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage develops the argument with a parallel from the debate on human action. The Mu'tazila object that one act cannot be from two agents, analogizing it to speech which cannot be from two speakers. The author cites the response of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, who distinguish between 'fi'l' (action) and 'kalam' (speech). 'Fi'l' is a general term for anything brought about by power, so it can be attributed to both God (as creator) and the servant (as acquirer). In contrast, 'kalam' is a specific type of act that requires a single locus because it negates silence and defect; thus it cannot be from two speakers. Similarly, 'hitting' (ضرب) is a specific type of act that causes pain and cannot receive pain, so it cannot be a hit object, but as a general act it can be created and thus be an effect of God. The author concludes by clarifying his own position: he denies that the same entity can be both an act and an effect from the same aspect, but affirms that something can be an act of the servant and an effect of God from different aspects.

Key Terms
فعل
مفعول
خلق
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidis believe the act is literally two different acts, one divine and one human.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that it is one act but considered from different aspects: as created by God and as acquired by the servant. There is no real multiplicity of acts.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does he deny regarding act and effect?
A) That an act can be an effect of God
B) That the same entity can be both act and effect from one aspect
C) That the servant has any real act
D) That God creates human acts

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position is that the servant's act is an effect of God and an act of the servant from different aspects, avoiding contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel pride attributing it to yourself, or do you recognize that Allah created that deed in you? How can you increase your humility and gratitude in light of this lesson?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. Do you ever feel that it is entirely your fault, forgetting that Allah created that act? Or do you use divine predestination as an excuse? How does the Maturidi distinction help you take responsibility while relying on Allah's mercy?
?
If every action you take is created by Allah, what does that mean for your sincerity (ikhlas)? Can you truly claim any act as your own? How should this affect your intentions and your hope for reward?

Act and Object: The Analogy of Hitting
The author uses the analogy of darb and madrub to distinguish act from object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an act (darb) and its object (madrub) and how this applies to the Maturidi doctrine of the creation of acts.

Core Idea
An act and its effect are distinct; only the effect, not the act itself, receives the attribute.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla uses the concrete example of hitting to clarify a subtle point in Maturidī kalām: how can an act be both created by God and performed by the servant? The author distinguishes between the act (ḍarb) and the one hit (maḍrūb), showing that the act itself does not receive the effect, though it is created. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Maturidī position on the creation of acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
2
Identify the key analogy
Focus on the relationship between ḍarb (hitting) and maḍrūb (the one hit). Notice how the author explains why they are not identical.
The parenthetical gives the reason: hitting does not accept pain.
3
Reflect on the theological conclusion
Consider the dual perspective: the act is servant's act yet God's object. Ask yourself how this preserves both human agency and divine creation.
This connects to the broader Maturidī teaching on kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذا الضرب عندنا لا يكون مضروبا (لأنه اسم لنوع فعل وهو الإيلام والمضروب اسم للمتألم والضرب لا يقبل الألم فلم يكن مشروبا) فأما الضرب فإنه يقبل التخليق فكان مخلوقا مفعولا، فكان من حيث إنه فعل للعبد مفعولا لله تعالى، فأما من حيث إنه ضرب لم يكن مضروبا لما والله الموفق. مر
Similarly, hitting (al-darb) in our view is not the one hit (madrub) (because it is a name for a type of act, which is causing pain, and the one hit is a name for the one who suffers pain, and hitting does not accept pain, so it is not the one hit). As for hitting, it accepts being created, so it is created and acted-upon. So it is, insofar as it is an act of the servant, an object of God Most High. But insofar as it is hitting, it is not the one hit. For that, and God is the Giver of success. Passed.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage presents an analogy between the general concept of act and object and the specific example of hitting (darb) and the one hit (madrub). The author argues that hitting is not the same as the one hit, because hitting is a type of act that causes pain, while the one hit is the recipient of pain. Hitting itself does not suffer pain, so it is not the one hit. However, hitting can be created, so it is a created object. From one perspective, hitting is an act of the servant and an object of God. From another perspective, as hitting, it is not the one hit. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
ضرب (ḍarb)
مضروب (maḍrūb)
إيلام (īlām)
تخليق (takhliq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act and its effect are identical because the act directly causes the effect.
Correction: The act (darb) is a different entity from its effect (madrub); the act itself does not receive the attribute (pain) that characterizes the effect.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is hitting (al-darb) not the one hit (al-madrub)?
A) Because hitting is an act while the one hit is a person.
B) Because hitting does not accept pain while the one hit does.
C) Because hitting is created while the one hit is not.
D) Because hitting is from God and the one hit from the servant.

Key Takeaway
Hitting (darb) is not the same as the one hit (madrub) because hitting does not experience pain, yet hitting is created by God as an act of the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions—like speaking, moving, or deciding—do you habitually think of them as entirely your own, or do you also acknowledge them as created by God at every moment? How would your daily sense of dependence on God change if you sincerely held this dual perspective?
?
In your life, have you ever mistakenly identified an act of yours (for example, a harsh word) with its effect (someone's pain) to the point that you felt you were directly responsible for their suffering, forgetting that the act and its object are distinct? How could this distinction lighten a heavy conscience or sharpen a mistaken assumption of control?
?
If hitting (darb) is not the one hit (madrub), then your identity is not defined by the actions you perform. What are you clinging to in your self-image that is really just an act, not your true self? Will you let God sift the acts from your being today?

Distinction Between Act and Object in Attribution
The author argues for necessary distinction between act and object in attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidis distinguish between an act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) in attribution, and why they deny their unification in one respect.

Core Idea
An act and its object cannot be identical for the same agent in the same respect.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle point: how can the same action be both an act and an object? He clarifies that the servant's act is an object for God (who creates it) and an act for the servant (who acquires it). This distinction preserves both divine power and human responsibility. The author emphasizes that he does not deny the two attributions; he only denies that they are unified in one respect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: فعل, مفعول, اتحاد, جهة, ذات.
The passage is dense but follows a logical order.
2
Identify the two main claims
The author first claims impossibility of act being object for the same agent. Then he denies unification (ittihad) of act and object. Understand the difference.
3
Apply the example
Consider the servant's act: for God it is an object (maf'ul), for the servant it is an act (fi'l). So what is an act for one is object for another. This supports the denial of unification for a single agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نحن إنما ندعي إحالة كون الفعل مفعولا لمن هو فعله، وإحالة كون المفعول فعلا لمن هو مفعوله وننكر اتحاد الفعل والمفعول وفيها ألزمت الأمر بخلافه، فإن فعل العبد عندنا كان مفعولا لله تعالى لا للعبد، وكان هو فعلا للعبد لا الله تعالى فمن كان هو فعلا له لم يكن مفعولا ، ومن كان مفعولا له لم يكن فعلا له ونحن لا ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر اتحاد الفعل والمفعول بجهة واحدة وهو إضافته إلى ذات واحد وبينا ذلك، وأثبتناه بتوفيق الله وعونه.
Then we indeed claim the impossibility of an act (al-fi'l) being an object (maf'ul) for the one for whom it is an act, and the impossibility of the object being an act for the one for whom it is an object. And we deny the unification (ittihad) of act and object. And in it, the matter was imposed contrary to that. For the act of the servant in our view is an object of God Most High, not of the servant, and it is an act of the servant, not of God Most High. So that which is an act for someone is not an object for him, and that which is an object for someone is not an act for him. And we do not deny this; we only deny the unification of act and object in one respect (jiha wahida), which is its attribution to one essence (dhat wahida). And we explained that and established it with the success and aid of God.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is a positive claim and a denial. The author asserts the impossibility of an act being an object for the same agent, and vice versa. He then denies any unification of act and object. He clarifies that the servant's act is an object for God (since God creates it) and an act for the servant (since the servant acquires it). Therefore, what is an act for someone cannot also be an object for that same person in the same respect. The author emphasizes that he does not deny that the act of the servant is an object for God and an act for the servant; he only denies that the act and object are unified in one respect, i.e., attributed to one essence. He concludes by stating that this has been explained and established with God's help.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l)
مفعول (maf'ul)
اتحاد (ittihad)
جهة (jiha)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidis claim that the act and object are the same thing.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are distinct in attribution: the servant's act is an object for God and an act for the servant, and they are never unified in one respect.

Checkpoint
What do Maturidis deny regarding the act and object according to this passage?
A) They deny that the act of the servant is created by God.
B) They deny the unification of act and object in one respect.
C) They deny that the servant has an act.
D) They deny that God has power.

Key Takeaway
The act and object of a single action are never unified in the same respect for one agent; they are always attributed to different agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you instinctively credit yourself or recognize that the act is simultaneously God's creation? How does this lesson challenge your sense of pride?
?
In a moment of sin, do you blame yourself or feel that God decreed it and thus avoid responsibility? How does the dual attribution—your act and God's object—call you to both repentance and trust in His mercy?
?
What area of your life do you most struggle to surrender to God's control while still taking full responsibility for your choices? What practical step can you take today to live out this distinction?

Causality and Created Things

Distinction Between Witnessed and Unseen in Act and Object
Al-Nasafi distinguishes between shahid and gha'ib regarding act and object, and criticizes Ash'ari reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's distinction between the witnessed and unseen worlds regarding the relationship between an act and its object, and how this critique of the Ash'ari position relies on consistent application of evidence.

Core Idea
In the unseen, an act can exist without its immediate effect; in the witnessed, it cannot.

This lesson explores al-Nasafi's argument that the witnessed (shahid) and unseen (gha'ib) realms operate under different rules regarding the relationship between an act and its object. He uses the example of a killing that only takes effect later to illustrate that in the unseen, the act can be valid even if its effect is delayed. He then criticizes the Ash'aris for inconsistently applying the evidence, reversing the proper distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the key distinction: act and object in shahid vs. gha'ib
Notice how al-Nasafi uses the example of killing (qatl): in the unseen (gha'ib), the act of throwing can be considered a killing even before the victim dies, whereas in the witnessed (shahid), the effect must be simultaneous. This is the core of the distinction.
The terms 'shahid' and 'gha'ib' are foundational in Maturidi kalam.
3
Understand al-Nasafi's criticism of the Ash'aris
Observe how al-Nasafi argues that the Ash'aris correctly applied the distinction in the case of command (amr) without commanded (ma'mur), but forgot it here. He accuses them of reversing the logical evidence: equating where differentiation is required and differentiating where equality is required.
This critique highlights the importance of consistency in applying rational evidence (dalil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يظهر عند انزهاق روحه أن ذلك الرمي من حين وجد كان قتلا وإن لم يوجد للحال أثره في المحل(١) وجد القتل ولا مقتول، ولهذا يظهر تعلق أحكام القتل بذلك حتى إن القتل لو كان خطأ فكفر كفارة القتل جاز، وإن كان المرمى إليه بعد حيا فكذا هذا، وفي فعل الشاهد لا يتصور حصول أثره (٢) الحقيقي بعد انعدامه، ففرقنا بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التفرقة. وبهذا فرقنا نحن والأشعرية في وجود الأمر ولا مأمور بين الشاهد والغائب وجوزنا ذلك في الغائب وقلنا بإحالته في الشاهد فعين هذا الفرق، فتذكرت الأشعرية هذا الفرق هناك ونسيت ها هنا. وجاء من هذا كله أن سوينا في الفعل والمفعول في إيجاب القول بتغايرهما بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التسوية، وفرقنا فيهما في حق جواز انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التفرقة. والأشعرية قلبت هذه القصة فسوت في ذلك بين الشاهد والغائب عند قيام دليل (التفرقة وفرقت بينهما عند قيام دليل التسوية ) (٣) وهذا قلب للمعقول وخروج عن قضية الدليل، والله الموفق. فإذا سلّمت هذه الطريقة (٤) وضح الاستدلال بالضرب والمضروب والقتل والمقتول وسائر الأفعال، واندفعت الأسئلة بحمد الله ومنه.
It appears when his soul departs that that throwing from the moment it occurred was a killing even if its effect did not appear in the place at the time. The killing existed but no killed person, and because of this the rulings of killing are attached to it, such that if the killing was accidental, then the expiation for the blood-money of killing is permissible, even if the one thrown at is still alive. Likewise this applies. And in the witnessed act, it is inconceivable that its real effect occurs after its annihilation. So we distinguished between the witnessed and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of differentiation. And by this we and the Ash'aris distinguished in the existence of a command without a commanded between the witnessed and the unseen, and we permitted that in the unseen and said it is impossible in the witnessed, and this is the same distinction. So the Ash'aris remembered this distinction there and forgot it here. And from all this came that we made equal in the act and the object in necessitating the statement of their mutual distinction between the witnessed and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of equality, and we differentiated between them regarding the permissibility of one being separate from the other between the witnessed and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of differentiation. And the Ash'aris reversed this story: they made equal in that between the witnessed and the unseen when there is evidence of differentiation, and they differentiated between them when there is evidence of equality. And this is a reversal of reason and a departure from the requirement of the evidence. And God is the grantor of success. If this method is granted, then the inference by striking and struck, killing and killed, and all actions becomes clear, and the questions are repelled by the praise of God and His grace.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument distinguishing between the witnessed (shahid) and unseen (gha'ib) realms in terms of the relationship between an act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul). Al-Nasafi uses the example of killing (qatl): in the unseen realm, an act can be validly attributed even if its effect is not immediately apparent (e.g., a fatal shot that only kills after a delay), whereas in the witnessed world, the effect must be simultaneous. He then explains that the Maturidis consistently apply this distinction: they affirm the possibility of a command without a commanded in the unseen but deny it in the witnessed, and they require a distinction between act and object in both realms but allow separation only in the unseen. The Ash'aris, he argues, reversed this logic—they equalize where evidence demands differentiation and differentiate where evidence demands equality. The passage concludes that once this method is accepted, all such inferences (e.g., striking, killing) become clear.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
فعل
مفعول
قتل
دليل التفرقة
دليل التسوية
أشعرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the correct rule regarding the relationship between act and object in the witnessed and unseen worlds?
A) Act and object are always inseparable in both worlds.
B) Act and object are always separable in both worlds.
C) In the witnessed, act and object must be simultaneous; in the unseen, separation is possible.
D) There is no difference between the witnessed and unseen in this regard.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi argues that rational evidence (dalil) demands distinguishing between witnessed and unseen in the relationship of act and object, and criticizes the Ash'aris for reversing this evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions in your life, do you ever assume that because you cannot see the immediate effect, the act is not real? How does al-Nasafi's example of killing challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for your patience in trials?
?
The Ash'aris are accused of forgetting their own principle when it comes to act and object. In what area of your faith do you hold to a standard in one situation but abandon it in another, and what would it cost you to be consistent?
?
If divine commands can exist without any commanded person in the unseen realm, what does that imply about the authority of Allah's commands over you even when you see no immediate obedience or result? Will you submit to His command today, regardless of visible outcomes?

Refutation of Bishr on Temporal Takwin
Nasafi refutes Bishr's view that temporal takwin needs no cause at all times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's reductio ad absurdum argument against Bishr's claim that temporal takwin requires a creator only at one moment.

Core Idea
If temporal takwin can exist without the made thing for one moment, then eternal takwin can too.

This passage presents a logical argument by Imam Nasafi against Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's position on takwin (bringing into existence). Bishr had argued that the temporal act of creation implies a creator only at the moment of creation, but Nasafi shows this leads to inconsistency. Through a reductio, he forces Bishr to accept that either the world needs no creator at all, or that an eternal takwin is possible. The argument is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand Bishr's Position
Bishr argued that the temporal takwin (the act of bringing into existence) only needs a creator at the moment it occurs. This implies that for all other times, the world could exist without a creator.
This is a Mu'tazili view that attempts to limit divine causality.
2
Read the Argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Nasafi's argument is a question: if takwin can exist without a cause for one moment, why not for all moments? And if it can exist without the made thing for one moment, why can't eternal takwin also exist without the made thing?
Notice the parallel structure: 'why not' and 'why is it permissible...'
3
Analyze the Logical Structure
Break down the argument into its logical steps. The commentary and guided breakdown will help you see the reductio: Bishr's own premises lead to a conclusion he would reject (that eternal takwin can exist without the world).
This shows inconsistency in Bishr's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لبشر بن المعتمر: إن التكوين الحادث الذي يتعلق وجود العالم به لما جاز وجوده بلا محدث زمانا لم لم يجز وجوده بدونه أزمنة، وهلا ذلك وجوده زمانا في حال عدم المكون على أنهما ليسا من المتلازمين لا محالة، وإذا جاز خلو التكوين الحادث عن اتصال المكون لم لا يجوز خلو التكوين القديم عن المكون؟
And it is said to Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir: The temporal takwin (bringing into existence) by which the existence of the world is related, since its existence without a cause was possible for one time, why was its existence not possible without it for all times? And why was its existence (possible) for one time in the state of the absence of the made thing, while they are not necessarily inseparable? And if it is permissible for the temporal takwin to lack connection to the made thing, why is it not permissible for the eternal takwin to lack the made thing?
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's position on takwin (bringing into existence). The author argues that if the temporal takwin—the act by which the world's existence comes about—can exist at one moment without a cause (since the opponent allegedly claims the world needs no cause for its existence at that moment), then why can it not exist without a cause for all moments? The author then points out that the temporal takwin can occur in the absence of the made thing (almukawwan), implying that the two are not necessarily inseparable. Given that, if a temporal takwin can lack connection to the made thing, why can an eternal takwin not also lack the made thing? The argument aims to show inconsistency in the opponent's reasoning.

Key Terms
التكوين الحادث
المكون
المتلازمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Nasafi is defending the eternity of the world.
Correction: Nasafi is not arguing for the world's eternity; rather, he is refuting a specific argument that would necessitate the world's eternity. He uses reductio to show that Bishr's premises lead to an unacceptable conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is Nasafi's main argument against Bishr regarding the temporal takwin?
A) The temporal takwin requires a creator at all times.
B) If the temporal takwin can lack the made thing in one time, it can lack it eternally.
C) The temporal takwin cannot exist without a cause.
D) The temporal takwin is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi forces Bishr to admit that if temporal takwin does not require a creator at all times, then eternal takwin does not require a made thing, exposing a flaw in the Mu'tazili position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you, like Bishr, instinctively assume that God's power must always be accompanied by its manifest effect—that if He is able to create, He must create at every moment? What does that assumption reveal about your understanding of God's self-sufficiency? Today, will you practice trusting in His ability even when you see no external result?
?
When you pray for something and do not see an immediate change, is there a part of you that doubts God's power or presence? How does Nasafi's point that takwin can exist without the made thing challenge your need for immediate outcomes? Will you commit to waiting patiently, knowing that God's action does not depend on your perception?
?
Bishr's error was to confine God's attribute of takwin to needing an object. Are there any attributes of God you have been limiting by your own expectations—such as His mercy, justice, or wisdom? Write down one specific way you have subconsciously bound God to your timetable, and resolve to repent from that mental restriction.

The Flaw in Analogizing from the Witnessed to the Unseen
Nasafi critiques the opponent's analogy by highlighting the difference in subsistence of the act in witnessed vs. unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the analogy between the transitory act in the witnessed world and the eternal divine attribute of takwin.

Core Idea
The act in the witnessed world is transient; divine takwin is eternal and subsisting.

Imam Nasafi exposes a critical methodological error: applying an analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen without accounting for fundamental differences. In the witnessed world, an act has no subsistence and perishes instantly if not related to an effect. But divine takwin is an eternal attribute that continues to subsist. This distinction invalidates the analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the argument settle before analyzing.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two sides of the analogy
The opponent analogizes from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). Find where Nasafi states this and note the terms used.
The terms 'fi'l' and 'maf'ul' are key.
3
Grasp the key difference in subsistence
Nasafi points out that in the witnessed world, the act has no subsistence (la baqa'a lahu). It perishes if not immediately related to an effect. In contrast, divine takwin must subsist until it relates to the mukawwan.
This is the core of the critique.
4
Understand why the analogy fails
Because the nature of the act in the two realms is fundamentally different, the analogy breaks down. The opponent's reasoning is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنك تعتبر ذلك بما تقرر في حسك ووهمك أن لا انفكاك في الشاهد بين الفعل والمفعول فتقيس عليه الغائب، وبينهما فرق على ما مر من وجوب بقاء التكوين إلى أن يتعلق بالمكون والفعل في الشاهد لا بقاء له، فلو لم يتعلق بالمفعول لفني من ساعته فلا يتصور تعلقه به البتة فلم يكن فعلا، وقد مر تقرير هذا الكلام قبل هذا.
Then you consider this by what is established in your sense and imagination: that there is no separation in the witnessed (al-shahid) between the act (al-fi'l) and the effect (al-maf'ul), so you analogize from it to the unseen (al-gha'ib). But there is a difference between them, as has passed: that it is necessary for al-takwin to subsist until it relates to al-mukawwan, whereas the act in the witnessed has no subsistence. For if it did not relate to the effect, it would perish immediately, so its relation to it cannot be conceived at all, and it would not be an act. And the exposition of this statement has passed before this.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage exposes a critical error in the opponent's methodology: they rely on an analogy from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib) without considering the difference. In the witnessed world, an act (e.g., writing) is transient and does not persist after the effect appears. Hence, the relation between act and effect in the witnessed world is simultaneous, and the act ceases once the effect exists. But in the divine case, takwin is an eternal attribute that continues to subsist even after it relates to the mukawwan. This key difference invalidates the analogy. The argument has been previously established.

Key Terms
shahid
gha'ib
fi'l
maf'ul

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is always valid because it is based on reason and observable patterns.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the analogy fails because the nature of the act differs: human acts are transient, while divine takwin is eternal and subsisting. This key difference breaks the analogical reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the analogy from the witnessed to the unseen regarding takwin?
A) Because the unseen is completely unknowable.
B) Because in the witnessed world, the act is transient, while divine takwin is eternal and subsists.
C) Because the opponent is an anthropomorphist.
D) Because the Quran explicitly forbids analogy.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi invalidates the analogy by demonstrating that the act in the witnessed world is transient, while divine takwin is eternal and subsisting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that your most reliable reasoning—based on what you see and experience—may be completely invalid when applied to God? What does that demand of your intellectual humility?
?

?
If the divine act of creating is eternal and subsisting, what does that imply about your own existence? Are you willing to see yourself as constantly being brought into being by a timeless act, rather than having been created once in the past?

The Interdependence of Substance and Accident
Nasafi proves necessary substance-accident tie via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that accidents cannot exist independently of substances, and why this refutes the possibility of eternal substances.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be alone; otherwise substances could be eternal.

Imam Nasafi presents a crisp logical proof that the existence of an accident always requires a substance in which it inheres. If this were not so, then substances could exist without accidents, leading to the absurdity that they might be eternal. This argument defends the contingency of the world and the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note the compact style of the argument.
The phrase 'قلب العقول' (inversion of intellects) signals absurdity.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: (1) Intellects reject separation of substance and accident. (2) If an accident could exist without a substance, then (by symmetry) a substance could exist without an accident. (3) That conclusion is irrational and leads to heresy.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the theological stakes
Ask why this matters: if substances could be accident-free, they could be eternal (qadim), challenging the doctrine that only Allah is eternal.
The Mu'tazila allowed self-subsistent accidents like will; Nasafi shows the consequence.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how the argument relies on the symmetry of possibility. Is the symmetry justified? This leads to deeper kalam discussions on causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه: أن الجواهر مع العرض مثلا زمان تأبى العقول انفراد أحدهما عن صاحبه، فلو جاز وجود عرض غير قائم بجوهر، لجاز وجود جوهر من غير قيام عرض به وهو قلب العقول وفتح باب القول بقدم الجواهر.
He verifies it: that substances with accidents, for example, at all times the intellects refuse the separation of one from its companion. For if the existence of an accident not subsisting in a substance were permissible, then the existence of a substance without an accident subsisting in it would be permissible, and that is an inversion of intellects and opening the door to the assertion of the pre-eternity of substances.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage presents a metaphysical argument based on the necessary correlation between substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad). The author states that reason (al-'uqul) always rejects the separation of a substance from its accident. If it were possible for an accident to exist independently without inhering in a substance (as the Mu'tazila claim for will), then by symmetry it would be possible for a substance to exist without any accident. This would 'invert the intellects'—meaning it is clearly irrational—and would open the door to claiming that substances are eternal (qadim), because if they can exist without accidental properties, they might not require a creator. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: the Mu'tazilite premise leads to an intolerable conclusion, so the premise is false. The passage follows a standard kalam argument form: stating the necessary link, then the conditional consequence, then the absurd result.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar) - substance
عرض ('arad) - accident
قدم (qidam) - pre-eternity
انفراد (infirad) - being alone, separation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument proves that accidents are eternal.
Correction: The argument actually proves the opposite: because the alternative (accidents independent) leads to eternal substances, which is false; so accidents must depend on substances that are created, avoiding eternity for both.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim would follow if an accident could exist without a substance?
A) Accidents would be eternal.
B) Substances could exist without accidents.
C) God would not exist.
D) Substances would become minds.

Key Takeaway
The necessary interdependence of substance and accident is a rational principle; breaking it leads to the heresy that matter could be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi says the intellect 'refuses' the separation of substance and accident. In your own life, when your intellect clearly sees a truth about Allah or His creation, do you submit to it or do you try to rationalize your way around it? What one truth are you currently resisting that you need to accept fully?
?
The argument warns that allowing a small break in the substance-accident tie opens the door to the pre-eternity of matter. Where in your faith practice are you allowing a 'small break'—a compromise you think is harmless—that could lead to larger errors? What step can you take today to restore that connection?
?
If your existence depends on your attributes (knowledge, life, character), then consider: Are you defined more by your 'substance' (who you are) or your 'accidents' (what you temporarily have)? How does the necessary tie between substance and accident challenge you to rely not on fleeting attributes but on your unchanging relationship with the Eternal?

Naturalists' Own Admission Supports Miracles
Proof from naturalists' own claim that all substances are one genus differing by accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the naturalists' own claim that all substances are one genus differing by accidents actually supports the possibility of miraculous transformation by God.

Core Idea
Naturalists' own admission undermines their denial of miracles.

The naturalists deny miracles like the transformation of natures, claiming they are impossible. However, the author reveals a contradiction in their position: they themselves admit that all substances belong to one genus and differ only by accidents, and that even fire can become water. This lesson explores how their own doctrine actually supports the possibility of miracles, exposing the weakness of their denial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Note the argument's flow from the denial of transformation to the naturalists' admission.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if... then... and what supports...
2
Identify the naturalists' key claim
Find where the author states what naturalists believe about substances and accidents. They say all substances are one genus and differ only by accidents.
The example of fire turning into water is given as something they admit.
3
See how this undermines their denial
Reflect on the contradiction: if change of accidents can transform fire into water, why deny that God can transform natures? The author calls their denial impudent.
The argument uses their own premise to refute their conclusion.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Understand that this is part of a proof for God's complete power. The passage shows that even naturalists' science supports divine omnipotence.
This lesson strengthens the concept of impossibility of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كان إنكار قلب الطبائع نتيجة القول بالتعطيل، وفيما سبق قيل: هذا من الدلائل على إثبات القدرة الكاملة لله تعالى ما يوجب بطلان قول هؤلاء الملحدين. والذي يؤيد بطلان هذه الشبهة: أن من قول جميع الطبيعيين أو الجمهور الأعظم منهم أن جواهر العالم كلها جنس واحد واختلافها بما يوجد فيها من الأعراض، وأن جوهر النار قد ينقلب ماء لتبدل كيفيتي الحرارة واليبوسة بالبرودة والرطوبة. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كيف ينكر جواز قلب الطبائع ويخرج من مقدور الله تعالى لولا الوقاحة والحماقة.
So if the denial of the transformation of natures is a result of the doctrine of negation, and previously it was said: This is among the proofs for establishing the complete power of God Almighty, which necessitates the falsehood of the statement of these atheists. And what supports the falsehood of this specious argument is that it is the statement of all naturalists, or the vast majority of them, that the substances of the world are all one genus, and their difference is due to the accidents found in them, and that the substance of fire can change into water due to the change of the two qualities of heat and dryness into cold and wetness. If the matter is so, how can one deny the permissibility of the transformation of natures and exclude it from the power of God Almighty, were it not for impudence and foolishness?
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage is a further refutation of the naturalists using their own doctrines. The author notes that the denial of miraculous transformation stems from a general negation of divine power (التعطيل). To support the invalidity of this specious argument, he cites the widely held position of naturalists that all substances in the world are of one single genus; they differ only by accidents (الأعراض). For example, the substance of fire can change into water if the qualities of heat and dryness are replaced by cold and wetness. This admission implies that natural transformation is possible. Therefore, it is absurd and impudent for them to deny that God can effect such transformations, since He is the creator of all substances and accidents.

Key Terms
الجوهر
العرض
قلب الطبائع
التعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naturalists deny all change, so they reject miracles.
Correction: Naturalists actually admit change of accidents (e.g., fire to water), so their denial of miraculous transformation is inconsistent with their own beliefs.

Checkpoint
What do naturalists admit according to the author that undermines their denial of miraculous transformation?
A) That substances are many different genera.
B) That fire can never become water.
C) That all substances are one genus differing only by accidents.
D) That God has limited power.

Key Takeaway
Naturalists' own belief that all substances are one genus supports the possibility of miraculous transformation by God.

The author accuses the deniers of 'impudence and foolishness.' Is there any area of your own life where you stubbornly deny Allah's control—perhaps in your finances, health, or plans—because you rely on 'natural causes'? How can you actively surrender that area to His power today?
?
If the naturalists themselves admit that substances can change through accidents, what does that imply about your own certainty that things will always remain as they are? What change in your relationship with Allah does this realization demand from you?

Refutation of Denying God's Power to Change Natures
Nasafi refutes the denial of God's power to change natural properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the denial of God's power to change natural properties by turning the natural philosophers' own theories against them.

Core Idea
If even naturalists admit substances can change, how can God's power be denied?

Nasafi argues that denying God's power to alter natures stems from a deeper problem: suspending God's attributes. But he cleverly turns the naturalists' own theories against them, showing that they already accept that substances can change through accidents. This makes their denial of divine power inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of refutation
Nasafi is responding to those who deny that God can change natural properties. This denial, he says, comes from ta'til (suspension of attributes). Notice how he frames the issue.
2
Examine the naturalists' own admission
Read the passage where Nasafi reports that most naturalists hold substances are one genus and differ by accidents. They even admit fire can become water. This is key.
3
Apply the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi asks: if they accept natural change, how can they deny God's power to cause such change? Only insolence and foolishness would lead to that conclusion. Reflect on the force of this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كان إنكار قلب الطبائع نتيجة القول بالتعطيل، وفيما سبق قيل: هذا من الدلائل على إثبات القدرة الكاملة لله تعالى ما يوجب بطلان قول هؤلاء الملحدين. والذي يؤيد بطلان هذه الشبهة: أن من قول جميع الطبيعيين أو الجمهور الأعظم منهم أن جواهر العالم كلها جنس واحد واختلافها بما يوجد فيها من الأعراض، وأن جوهر النار قد ينقلب ماء لتبدل كيفيتي الحرارة واليبوسة بالبرودة والرطوبة. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كيف ينكر جواز قلب الطبائع ويخرج من مقدور الله تعالى لولا الوقاحة والحماقة.
So if the denial of changing natures is a result of the affirmation of suspension (taʿṭīl), and in what has preceded it was said: This is among the evidences for establishing the perfect power (qudra) of God Most High which necessitates the invalidity of the statement of these atheists. And that which supports the invalidity of this doubt is: that among the statements of all the naturalists (ṭabīʿiyyīn) or the vast majority of them is that the substances (jawāhir) of the world are all of one genus (jins wāḥid) and their difference is due to what is found in them of accidents (aʿrāḍ), and that the substance of fire may turn into water due to the change of the qualities of heat and dryness to cold and humidity. And if the matter is thus, how can one deny the permissibility of changing natures and exclude it from the capacity (maqdūr) of God Most High, without insolence and foolishness.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny that God can change natural properties (قلب الطبائع). Nasafi argues that such denial stems from a position of suspending God's attributes (التعطيل). He first reminds the reader that earlier (in the text) it was established that the denial of miracles implies a rejection of God's perfect power, which invalidates the atheists' claim. Then he provides a supporting argument: even natural philosophers—or at least the vast majority of them—hold that all substances in the world are fundamentally of one genus, and their differences arise from accidents such as heat, cold, dryness, and humidity. They admit that fire can turn into water when its qualities change. Since these naturalists themselves accept the possibility of substantial change through natural causes, how can they deny that God, who is far more powerful, can cause such changes? Nasafi concludes that only insolence and ignorance would lead one to exclude such acts from God's power.

القدرة الكاملة (perfect power)

جوهر (substance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles necessarily violate fixed laws of nature that cannot change.
Correction: Nasafi shows that even naturalists accept that substances can change through accidents (e.g., fire becoming water), so denying God's power to cause such change is inconsistent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the denial of God's power to change natures by showing that even naturalists accept that substances can change through accidents, making their denial contradictory.

Defending Bodily Resurrection: Objections and Answers
Nasafi presents and refutes two philosophical objections to bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main philosophical objections to bodily resurrection and how Nasafi refutes them using rational arguments based on original parts and divine purpose.

Core Idea
The resurrection of bodies is rational and necessary, even against philosophical objections.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles head-on the most common philosophical arguments against bodily resurrection. He first establishes that all religions agree on its possibility and occurrence, as established by prophetic testimony. Then he presents two key objections from the philosophers: the cannibalism paradox and the problem of purpose. His refutations rely on the distinction between original and transient body parts, and the rejection of assigning a limited purpose to God's actions. Though the text cuts off, the first refutation is complete and the second is partially given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: introduction, then permissibility and occurrence, then two objections.
The text cuts off mid-sentence in the second answer.
2
Identify the two objections
Locate the 'first' (الأول) and 'second' (الثاني) arguments. Summarize each objection in your own words.
3
Analyze the refutations
See how Nasafi responds to each objection. For the first, he introduces the concept of 'original parts' (الأجزاء الأصلية). For the second, he challenges the assumption that a purpose must be pain or pleasure.
The second refutation is incomplete; think about what might complete it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام في صورة الحشر والثواب ففيه بين الناس اختلاف كبير، وفي الأقاويل وشبهها وكيفية وجوه ردها وتحقيق ما أتى به الرسول صلوات جميع الله عليه طول فأعرضنا عن ذكرها. بيان: بالنسبة للحشر ووقوعه بالأجسام فقد أجمع أهل الملل على جواز الحشر ووقوعه. أما الفلاسفة فقد أنكروا حشر الأجساد وأنكروا الوقوع أما الجواز - عند من جوز - فلأن جمع الأجزاء وإعادة التأليف فيها أمر ممكن، والله عالم بتلك الأجزاء قادر على جمعها وتأليفها لعموم علمه وقدرته، وصحة القبول والفعل توجب الصحة قطعًا. وأما الوقوع فلأن الصادق أخبر عنه في مواضع لا تحصى بعبارات لا تقبل التأويل حتى صار معلومًا بالضرورة كونه من الدين، وكل ما أخبر به الصادق فهو حق، والمنكرون يحتجون بأمرين: الأول: لو أكل إنسان إنسانا بحيث صار المأكول جزءًا منه فتلك الأجزاء إما أن تعاد فيهما، وهو محال، أو فى أحدهما فلا يكون الآخر معادًا بعينه، ويجاب عن هذا بأن المعاد هو الأجزاء الأصلية وهي الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، وهذه في الأكل فضل فإنا نعلم أن الإنسان باق مدة عمره وأجزاء الغذاء تتوارد عليه وتزول عنه. الثاني: لو حشر فإما لا لغرض وهو عبث، وإما لغرض، وهو إما عائد إلى الله وهو منزه عنه، أو إلى العبد، وهو إما الإيلام وإنه منتف إجماعًا وببديهة العقل لقبحه وعدم ملاءمته والعناية، وإما الإلذاذ وهو أيضا باطل لأن اللذة إنما هي دفع الألم بالاستقراء وإنه للحكمة لو ترك لم يكن له ألم والإيلام ليدفع فيلتذ لا يصلح غرضا إذ لا معنى له. ويجاب عن هذا أنا نختار أنه لا لغرض ولا عبث ولا قبح عقلي على الله تعالى - كذلك لا نسلم أن الغرض هو إما الإيلام أو الإلذاذ ولعل فيه غرضا آخر لا نعلمه. ثم سلمنا لكن لا نسلم أن اللذة دفع الألم، غايته أن في دفع الألم لذة.. ثم سلمنا أن ذلك في اللذات الدنيوية فلم قلتم إن اللذات الأخروية كذلك، ولم لا يجوز أن
As for the discussion on the form of the gathering and reward, there is great difference among people regarding it. And regarding the statements and their likes and the manner of refuting them and verifying what the messenger (peace be upon them all) brought, we have refrained from mentioning them. Explanation: Regarding the gathering and its occurrence with bodies, the people of religions have agreed on the permissibility and occurrence of the gathering. As for the philosophers, they denied the resurrection of bodies and denied the occurrence. As for permissibility - according to those who deem it permissible - it is because gathering the parts and re-establishing the composition is possible. God knows those parts and is able to gather and compose them due to the universality of His knowledge and power. The soundness of acceptance and action necessarily necessitates soundness. As for occurrence, it is because the truthful one informed about it in countless places with expressions that do not admit interpretation, such that it became known necessarily as part of the religion. Everything the truthful one informed about is true. The deniers argue with two things: First: If a person eats another person so that the eaten becomes part of him, then those parts are either returned in both (which is impossible) or in one of them, so the other would not be returned as the same entity. The answer to this is that what is returned are the original parts, which remain from the beginning of life to its end. These (in the case of eating) are surplus. We know that a person remains throughout his lifespan while the parts of nutrition come and go. Second: If he gathers (resurrects), it is either for no purpose, which is absurd, or for a purpose. That purpose either returns to God - who is transcendent above it - or to the servant. It is either for causing pain, which is unanimously rejected and by the intuition of reason due to its ugliness and lack of suitability and care, or for giving pleasure, which is also invalid because pleasure is only the removal of pain, inductively. For wisdom, if left, he would have no pain, and causing pain to remove it in order to derive pleasure is not a valid purpose because it has no meaning. The answer to this is that we choose that it is without purpose, and there is no absurdity or rational ugliness regarding God Almighty. Likewise, we do not concede that the purpose is either causing pain or giving pleasure; perhaps there is another purpose we do not know. Then we concede, but we do not concede that pleasure is the removal of pain; at most, there is pleasure in the removal of pain. Then we concede that this applies to worldly pleasures; why do you claim that otherworldly pleasures are the same? And why is it not permissible that...
Source pages p. 766

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the resurrection of bodies. The author first notes that there is disagreement about the form of the gathering (hashr) and reward. He then provides a brief summary: all religious communities (ahl al-milal) agree on the permissibility and occurrence of bodily resurrection, while philosophers deny both. The author explains that permissibility is based on God's knowledge and power to reassemble parts. Occurrence is based on the truthful informant (the Prophet) who conveyed it in unequivocal terms. Then he presents two objections from the deniers. The first objection concerns the case of cannibalism: if a person eats another, the parts cannot be returned to both bodies simultaneously, leading to impossibility. The answer distinguishes between original parts (which persist throughout life) and nutritional parts (which are transient). The second objection concerns purpose: resurrection without purpose is absurd, and if for a purpose, it must be either for God (impossible) or for the servant, either for pain (which is unjust) or for pleasure (which is merely removal of pain). The answer refutes that any purpose must be either pain or pleasure, and argues that God is not bound by such purposes, and that resurrection may have another purpose we do not know. The argument continues, stating that the concept of pleasure may differ in the afterlife. The text ends mid-argument, but the main points are covered.

Key Terms
حشر الأجساد
الأجزاء الأصلية
عبث
غرض
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodily resurrection is impossible because if a person eats another, the parts cannot be restored to both.
Correction: Nasafi argues that only the original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya) are resurrected; digestive parts are transient and not essential to personal identity.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against bodily resurrection mentioned in the passage?
A) That God cannot resurrect bodies because they decompose
B) That if a person eats another, the same parts cannot be returned to both
C) That resurrection serves no purpose
D) That pleasure is only the removal of pain

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends bodily resurrection by distinguishing original body parts from transient ones and by rejecting anthropomorphic purpose attribution to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own body returning to dust and then being recreated by Allah, do you feel the same certainty as the author? If there is any doubt in your heart, what is its source? Commit to memorizing the proofs from this passage so that you can answer your own uncertainties.
?
The author defends resurrection by saying that God's actions are not bound by human notions of purpose. Are there areas of your life where you secretly wish Allah would act according to your sense of justice or meaning? How can you train yourself to submit to His wisdom even when you do not understand?
?
Resurrection is a certainty for every believer. Does your daily schedule reflect that you truly expect to stand before Allah? Name one concrete habit you will change today to live with that reality, such as praying with mindfulness or giving in charity.

Two Meanings of Istita'a in the Quran
Distinguishes istita'at al-alat from haqiqat al-qudra with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of 'istiṭāʿa' in the Quran—capacity of instruments and reality of power—and how they are distinguished through Quranic evidence and theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Quranic 'ability' sometimes means external means, sometimes real power—only the latter incurs blame.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla tackles a critical semantic distinction in the Quran: the term 'istiṭāʿa' (capacity/ability) carries two very different meanings depending on context. Al-Nasafī, the author, shows that common people often confuse them, yet theologians need this distinction to correctly understand divine reproach and human responsibility. Let's explore the evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author introduces the problem of commoners' ignorance, then presents two categories of verses.
The passage uses a lot of Arabic terminology; refer to the key terms list.
2
Identify the two meanings
Look for the two distinct meanings of istiṭāʿa: (1) capacity of instruments (istiṭāʿat al-ālāt) like provision and mount for Hajj, and (2) reality of power (ḥaqīqat al-qudra) for action itself.
The first meaning is illustrated by two verses; the second by verses about hearing and patience.
3
Understand the argument about blame
Notice the reasoning: reproach only attaches when means are present but real power is absent due to one's own negligence. The story of Moses and al-Khiḍr reinforces this: Moses had the instruments of patience but lacked its reality.
This connects to the broader kalām discussion of human action and responsibility.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can distinguish the two meanings. Then try the activity to reinforce the argument about blame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أن أهل النفاق كانوا عوام، وقدرة العمل التي توجب حصول الفعل ويتكلم فيها المتكلمون أنها مع الفعل أو قبله وتبقى أو لا تبقى مما لا يعرفه العوام ولا يصورونها في الأوهام، وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلًا ﴾ والمراد به استطاعة الآلات، وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلِلَّهِ عَلَى النَّاسِ حِجُّ الْبَيْتِ مَنِ اسْتَطَاعَ إِلَيْهِ سَبِيلًا ﴾ والمراد به الزاد والراحلة لا حقيقة قدرة الفعل، فهذه الآيات دليل ثبوت استطاعة الأسباب والآلات.

هناك استطاعة بمعنى حقيقة القدرة:
وأما دليل ثبوت الاستطاعة التي هي حقيقة القدرة فقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا كَانُوا يَسْتَطِيعُونَ السَّمْعَ ﴾ والمراد منه نفي حقيقة القدرة لا نفي الأسباب والآلات؛ لأنها كانت ثابتة، وإنما المنتفى عنهم كان حقيقة القدرة التي بها يتعلق الفعل.

يحققه أنه ذكر ذلك على وجه الذم لهم، والذم إنما يلحقهم بانعدام حقيقة القدرة عند وجود الأسباب وصحة الآلات لا بانعدام سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، لأن انتفاء تلك الاستطاعة لم تكن بتضييعه بل هو في ذلك مجبور، انتفاء حقيقة القدرة فموجب ذمهم؛ لأن انعدامها مع سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات كانت بتضييعه إياها لاشتغاله بضد ما أمر به.

يحققه أنه خص بنفس هذه الاستطاعة الكافر، وانتفاء تلك الاستطاعة يستوي فيه المسلم والكافر، وإنما المختص بالكافر هو انتفاء هذه الاستطاعة، والدليل عليه قول صاحب موسى لموسى عليهما السلام: ﴿ إِنَّكَ لَنْ تَسْتَطِيعَ مَعِيَ صَبْرًا ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ أَلَمْ أَقُلْ إِنَّكَ لَنْ تَسْتَطِيعَ مَعِيَ صَبْرًا ﴾ والمراد منه حقيقة قدرة الصبر لا أسباب الصبر والآلة فإن تلك كانت ثابتة له.

ألا ترى أنه عاتبه على ذلك ولا يلام من عدم آلات الفعل وأسبابه وإنما يلام من امتنع منه الفعل لتضييعه قدرة الفعل لاشتغاله بغير ما أمر به، وشغله إياها بضد ما أمر به والله الموفق.
He verifies that the people of hypocrisy were commoners, and the power of action which necessitates the occurrence of the act, and about which the theologians speak—whether it is with the act or before it, and whether it remains or does not remain—is something that the commoners do not know nor picture in their minds. That is His statement: 'And whoever among you does not have the means (yastaṭiʿ)'—and what is meant by it is the capacity of instruments (istiṭāʿat al-ālāt). Likewise His statement: 'And due to Allah from the people is a pilgrimage to the House—for whoever has the ability (istaṭāʿa) to find a way to it'—and what is meant by it is provision and mount, not the reality of the power of the act. So these verses are evidence for the existence of the capacity of causes and instruments.

There is a capacity in the sense of the reality of power (ḥaqīqat al-qudra):
As for the evidence for the existence of the capacity that is the reality of power, it is His statement: 'They were not able (yastaṭīʿūna) to hear'—and what is meant by it is the negation of the reality of power, not the negation of causes and instruments; because they were present, and what was negated from them was the reality of power by which the act is connected.

He verifies that He mentioned that as a reproach against them, and reproach only attaches to them due to the absence of the reality of power in the presence of causes and soundness of instruments, not due to the absence of soundness of causes and instruments. Because the absence of that capacity was not by their negligence, rather they were compelled in that; the absence of the reality of power is what makes reproach binding upon them, because its absence with soundness of causes and instruments was due to their wasting it by occupying themselves with the opposite of what they were commanded.

He verifies that this very capacity is specific to the disbeliever, while the absence of that capacity applies equally to the Muslim and the disbeliever. What is specific to the disbeliever is the absence of this capacity. The evidence for it is the statement of the companion of Moses to Moses, peace be upon them: 'Indeed, you will never be able (lan tastaṭīʿa) to be patient with me' and his statement: 'Did I not say that you will never be able (lan tastaṭīʿa) to be patient with me?'—and what is meant by it is the reality of the power of patience, not the causes of patience and the instrument, for those were present for him.

Do you not see that he reproached him for that? And one is not blamed for the absence of the instruments of the act and its causes; rather one is blamed for refraining from the act because he wasted the power of the act by occupying himself with other than what he was commanded, and by occupying it with the opposite of what he was commanded. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the term 'istiṭāʿa' (capacity/ability) has two distinct meanings in the Quran: (1) the capacity of means and instruments (istiṭāʿat al-ālāt), such as physical health, provision, or tools, and (2) the reality of power (ḥaqīqat al-qudra), which is the power of action itself. The author begins by noting that common people are unaware of the theological debates about this power—whether it accompanies the act, precedes it, or remains. He then cites two verses where 'istiṭāʿa' clearly refers to external means: the verse about marrying free believing women if one does not have the means (tawl), and the verse about pilgrimage requiring the ability (sabīl) of provision and transport. These support the existence of a capacity related to causes and instruments. Next, he turns to the second meaning: true power. He cites the verse 'They were not able to hear' (Hūd 11:20), arguing that this negation cannot refer to causes and instruments, since those were present for the disbelievers; rather, it negates the reality of power by which an act is performed. The author then explains that reproach for disbelief is only valid if the person had sound instruments and causes but lacked the actual power due to his own negligence—i.e., he wasted his capacity by engaging in the opposite of what he was commanded. He further illustrates the distinction with the story of Moses and al-Khiḍr: when al-Khiḍr said 'You will never be able to be patient with me,' he was referring to the reality of the power of patience, not the external causes, because Moses had those. The conclusion is that blame attaches only to the absence of real power when means are present, because that absence is due to the person's own misuse.

قدرة
آلات
أسباب
حقيقة القدرة
استطاعة الآلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Quranic mentions of 'istiṭāʿa' refer to the same kind of ability, either always external means or always inner power.
Correction: The passage shows that the Quran uses 'istiṭāʿa' in two distinct senses: sometimes it means external means (like provision for Hajj), and sometimes it means the reality of power itself (like the ability to hear guidance). Recognizing which is used is crucial for understanding divine reproach.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between the two meanings of 'istiṭāʿa'?
A) One refers to physical strength, the other to wealth.
B) One is capacity through external means/instruments, the other is the reality of power itself.
C) One applies to believers, the other to disbelievers.
D) One is eternal, the other is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The term 'istiṭāʿa' in the Quran carries two distinct meanings—capacity of instruments and reality of power—and the latter alone is the basis for divine reproach when means are present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to carry out a religious duty, do you instinctively point to external obstacles (lack of time, resources, etc.) or do you honestly examine whether you have wasted your inner capacity by engaging in the opposite of what Allah commands? How can you shift your focus today?
?
The lesson says the disbelievers were reproached because they had sound instruments but wasted real power by occupying themselves with the opposite of what they were commanded. In your life, where are you occupying yourself with the opposite of what Allah wants while having the means to obey? What step will you take to reclaim that capacity?
?
Consider the story of Moses and al-Khiḍr: Moses had all the external means to be patient but lacked the reality of patience. Where in your life do you have the outward ability to do good (health, wealth, time) but lack the inner resolve? What would it take for you to cultivate that real power, starting now?

Proof That Istita'a Is an Accident
Refutes denial of istita'a and proves it as an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence that istita'a (capacity) is an accident separate from the agent and how it refutes the views of certain Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
Capacity is an accident that increases without physical change.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a key argument in the debate on human capacity. He refutes the position that man is capable by himself without an additional attribute. Through empirical observation and a clever analogy, he demonstrates that capacity (istiṭāʿa) is an accident existing beyond the essence. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Notice how it begins with a refutation, then asserts the claim, provides evidence, and ends with another refutation.
The passage is concise but packed with argument.
2
Identify the refuted positions
The passage refutes two groups: those who deny any separate istita'a (al-Nazzam, al-Uswari, al-Asamm) and Ghaylan and his followers. Understand their core claim: that man is capable by himself without an accident of capacity.
These are Mu'tazilite views.
3
Analyze the proof and analogy
Examine the empirical proof: a person can carry 50 and then 100 pounds without change in limbs. Then analyze the analogy of the twisted threads. Understand how these demonstrate that capacity is an accident that can vary.
The analogy shows that a twist (fatl) is an accident that adds difficulty without increasing parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل بهذا قول من يقول: لا استطاعة للإنسان إذ هي ليست معنى وراء المستطيع بل الإنسان مستطيع بنفسه لا باستطاعة كما ذهب إليه النظام وعلى الأسواري وأبو بكر الأصم.

الاستطاعة عرض وراء الذات

لأنا بينا بالدليل ثبوتها وهي عرض من الأعراض، ولا شك أن العرض معنى وراء الجسم، والذي يدل على ثبوتها أنا وجدنا الإنسان سليم الجوارح ليس بذي آفة وهو قادر على حمل خمسين رطلاً، ثم وجدناه في حالة أخرى قادرا على حمل مائة رطل من غير زيادة في أجزاء أعضائه، ونظيره خيطان منشوران يصعب قطعهما، وإذا فتلا يصعب القطع من غير زيادة في أجزاء الخيطين بل لحدوث الفتل وهو عرض في نفسه، وبهذا يبطل أيضًا قول غيلان وأتباعه.
And by this is refuted the statement of whoever says: There is no capacity (istiṭāʿa) for man; rather it is not a meaning beyond the capable one (al-mustaṭīʿ), but man is capable by himself, not by a capacity—as held by al-Naẓẓām, ʿAlī al-Uswārī, and Abū Bakr al-Aṣamm.

Capacity is an accident (ʿaraḍ) beyond the essence (al-dhāt):
For we have proved by evidence its existence, and it is an accident among accidents, and there is no doubt that the accident is a meaning beyond the body. And what indicates its existence is that we find a man with sound limbs, not having any defect, able to carry fifty pounds; then we find him in another state able to carry one hundred pounds without any increase in the parts of his limbs. Its analogy is two twisted threads which it is difficult to cut, but when they are twisted together it becomes difficult to cut without any increase in the parts of the two threads, but due to the occurrence of twisting (al-fatl) which is an accident in itself. And by this is also refuted the statement of Ghaylān and his followers.
Source pages p. 789

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that there is no additional entity of capacity (istiṭāʿa) beyond the agent himself. The author identifies this position with the Muʿtazilites al-Naẓẓām, ʿAlī al-Uswārī, and Abū Bakr al-Aṣamm, who held that man is 'capable by himself' without a separate accident. The author then asserts that istiṭāʿa is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that exists beyond the essence (dhāt). The proof is empirical: a person with healthy limbs can carry 50 pounds, and later the same person can carry 100 pounds without any change in the size or number of limbs. The increase in capacity must therefore be due to an additional accident. An analogy is given: two separate threads are easy to cut, but when twisted together they become harder to cut, even though the number of parts has not increased; the difficulty arises from the twist (fatl), which is an accident. This same reasoning refutes the position of Ghaylān and his followers, who likely denied the reality of capacity as an attribute.

Key Terms
استطاعة
عرض
ذات
قدرة
مستطيع
فتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capacity is just a disposition of the body and does not exist as a separate accident.
Correction: The passage shows that capacity can increase without physical change, proving it is an additional accident beyond the body.

Checkpoint
What is the main proof given in the passage for capacity (istiṭāʿa) being an accident?
A) Quranic verses about pilgrimage.
B) Consensus of scholars.
C) The observation that a person can carry different weights without change in limb size.
D) The analogy of a cut thread.

Key Takeaway
Capacity (istiṭāʿa) is an accident that can increase without physical change, refuting those who deny it as a separate attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself capable of something today that you were not capable of yesterday, do you attribute that change to Allah's creation of an accident in you, or do you take pride in your own essence? How does this lesson change how you thank Allah for your abilities?
?
If capacity is an accident that can increase without physical change, then what is stopping you from seeking more capacity for good deeds? Are you relying on your own essence or begging Allah for accidental increases?
?
The passage shows that even physical strength can increase without visible change. In what area of your spiritual life do you need an accidental increase from Allah that only He can grant? Will you ask Him tonight?
